  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f1dbc4d8-62cb-4255-bdb7-ad308beaa398'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17390266814475383097">From Pulitzer Prize finalist Linda Hogan,<i> Solar Storms </i>tells the moving, “luminous” (<i>Publishers Weekly</i>) story of Angela Jenson, a troubled Native American girl coming of age in the foster system in Oklahoma, who decides to reunite with her family.<br/><br/>At seventeen, Angela returns to the place where she was raised—a stunning island town that lies at the border of Canada and Minn</span>
  <span id="freeText17390266814475383097" style="display:none">From Pulitzer Prize finalist Linda Hogan,<i> Solar Storms </i>tells the moving, “luminous” (<i>Publishers Weekly</i>) story of Angela Jenson, a troubled Native American girl coming of age in the foster system in Oklahoma, who decides to reunite with her family.<br /><br />At seventeen, Angela returns to the place where she was raised—a stunning island town that lies at the border of Canada and Minnesota—where she finds that an eager developer is planning a hydroelectric dam that will leave sacred land flooded and abandoned. Joining up with three other concerned residents, Angela fights the project, reconnecting with her ancestral roots as she does so.<br /> <br />Harrowing, lyrical, and boldly incisive, <i>Solar Storms </i>is a powerful examination of the clashes between cultures and traumatic repercussions that have shaped American history.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17390266814475383097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/608661?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Colin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Colin" href="/user/show/51647-colin">Colin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51647-colin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51647-colin?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51647-colin?shelf=disabilitystudies">disabilitystudies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51647-colin?shelf=genderstudies">genderstudies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51647-colin?shelf=crymyfaceoff">crymyfaceoff</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer608661" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10455800928971339041">Quite possibly my favorite book ever. It's definitely in the top five. It's an intense story of family, love, change, healing from abuse, and decolonization. Linda Hogan's writing is exquisite--it's obvious that she's also a gifted poet with each paragraph. I remember the first time I finished reading this, I was sitting in the atrium at the public library, clutching the book to my chest and sobbing while total strangers were uncomfortably shooting sidelong glances my way. It's that beautiful. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_168720375" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168720375" />
    <a title="Margaret Murray" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5011503-margaret-murray"><img alt="Margaret Murray" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442474359p2/5011503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/168720375?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margaret Murray" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margaret Murray" href="/user/show/5011503-margaret-murray">Margaret Murray</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer168720375" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer233593037676626471">I'm a novel writer who recently found Solar Storms (published in '95) through the local Copperfield Bookstore Club's backlisted picks in Sebastopol, CA where I live. <br/><br/>I was overwhelmed, amazed and entranced as I read Solar Storms especially so since it was written some time ago. I felt a horror of recognition. To my mind, the terrible hydroelectric dam project created in the novel that was challenged by the Native American women echoed the destruction and tragedy that has just recently befallen J</span>
  <span id="freeText233593037676626471" style="display:none">I'm a novel writer who recently found Solar Storms (published in '95) through the local Copperfield Bookstore Club's backlisted picks in Sebastopol, CA where I live. <br /><br />I was overwhelmed, amazed and entranced as I read Solar Storms especially so since it was written some time ago. I felt a horror of recognition. To my mind, the terrible hydroelectric dam project created in the novel that was challenged by the Native American women echoed the destruction and tragedy that has just recently befallen Japan (and the earth) as a result of the tsunami destroying the Fukushima nuclear plant. <br /><br />In both I see the stupidity and greed of mankind, the power and revenge of water, the subversion of nature, and the destruction of wildlife. <br /><br />I imagine the insidious poison of radiation floating over our earth now as paralleling the inhuman aggression of the colonizing government that the Native American and activist characters fight in Linda Hogan's novel. <br /><br />It's all there in Solar Storms. As the water is poisoned and misdirected, so are we and the earth itself. Yet the vision brings me hope. Angel, the young, abused heroine, embodies that hope. <br /><br />Like the beautiful, disfigured Angel desperate to reimagine her doomed mother, I paddled the canoe of this story into a deep part of my own nature, my own family. I see my own life and my work in a new way--the way Angel's paddle strokes bring her closer to home, the way Dora Rouge finds love as an old woman, the way Bush publishes her fighting words to the world.<br /><br />This is a brilliant story of long endured injustices that burn like nuclear waste, describing the natural world and the poor, strange, unique women who refuse to give it up.<br /><br />Margaret C. Murray<br />writewordspress.com<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="233593037676626471" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_311673640" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/311673640" />
    <a title="David Grant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3443302-david-grant"><img alt="David Grant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1268704488p2/3443302.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/311673640?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Grant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Grant" href="/user/show/3443302-david-grant">David Grant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer311673640" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18178028925159063182">Awesome!!! Simply a delight to read. It touches on so many themes: nature/ ecology, Native American rights and history, spirituality, feminism, and community. It is deeply lyrical and thought-provoking in a quiet, rumbling way like a distant thunderstorm approaching over the horizon.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

          </span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1104071-leah?shelf=books-read-2017">books-read-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2158685006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8479760142942433115">What a gorgeous book! I cannot remember when I have ever underlined so many passages in a book just because they were so beautifully written that I wanted to be able to find them again easily.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1749371057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10018589559068974902">Solar Storms is one of the deepest and most transcendent books I have ever read. Hogan's writing is pure poetry. The inner experience her characters have of the world, the earth, of our lost connection to "place" has changed the way I see and feel my connection to those things as well. Her characters live on inside you long after you have read the last page. I couldn't recommend it more highly. It is now my "favorite" book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2354918229" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2354918229" />
    <a title="Olivia Feliciano" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/80486569-olivia-feliciano"><img alt="Olivia Feliciano" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2354918229?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olivia Feliciano" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olivia Feliciano" href="/user/show/80486569-olivia-feliciano">Olivia Feliciano</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2354918229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8365004788463295120">Fighting for Nature and Finding Family<br/><br/>	Self-discovery is a struggle that many individuals deal with. This proved to be the case for the main character of Solar Storms by Linda Hogan. Growing up in the system, Angel bounced from home to home, longing for a deeper connection to her family. She relentlessly searched for her biological mother, Hannah Wing. What her search procured was more than she could have hoped for; she found three loving grandmothers, a supportive community, a land of natural w</span>
  <span id="freeText8365004788463295120" style="display:none">Fighting for Nature and Finding Family<br /><br />	Self-discovery is a struggle that many individuals deal with. This proved to be the case for the main character of Solar Storms by Linda Hogan. Growing up in the system, Angel bounced from home to home, longing for a deeper connection to her family. She relentlessly searched for her biological mother, Hannah Wing. What her search procured was more than she could have hoped for; she found three loving grandmothers, a supportive community, a land of natural wonder, and a sense of belonging like she had never known.<br /><br />	Without a family or a known past, Angel spent her time trying to track down her mother and searching for a connection to something meaningful in life. Meeting Agnes Iron, Dora-Rouge, and Bush, her grandmothers, and experiencing life at Adam’s Rib and Fur Island, Angel recognized that she had found what she had spent her life searching for, “this older world…only [her] body remembered” (Hogan 79). She had always felt a connection to the land of her people, but she did not understand what it meant until she entered the world of her grandmothers. As the land that she and her matriarchal family fought to save continued to be ripped apart by the western world, Angel was able to piece herself together. The more time spent with her people, the more Angel felt accepted as she learned of her plant-dreaming abilities and began to embrace her soul-deep connection to the land. Throughout the journey, readers witness Angel develop into a young woman with an unshakeable sense of belonging in this matriarchal world of origin.<br /><br />	The story is told in a first-person point of view with Angel as the main character and narrator. The point of view allows for a more intimate look into Angel’s self-discovery journey. As the reader is introduced to this new world, so is the narrator. This allows the story to be authentic and inviting, which makes readers more invested in the lives of the characters. The first-hand account of the story also allows for a better understanding of how Angel develops and matures.<br /><br />	The setting in Solar Storms is a driving force for Hogan’s storyline. What better way to advocate for environmental conservation than to write an entire book that revolves around it. Land and nature were pivotal parts of the people’s lives. This was most evident when Dora-Rouge returned to her home land and witnessed the despair and lifelessness of the land and the Fat-Eaters. Because the people lived in harmony with the land, they suffered when the land suffered. The land, however, was not easily destroyed. It downed the developers’ precious light poles, swallowed the structures that defiled its surface, and melted the road that threatened to suffocate it. As Angel said, it was a native survivor, like herself and her community (Hogan 224). Throughout the course of the book, the humans’ lives reflected the seasons and the state of the land. Autumn was busy with preparation, winter was frozen still, spring was buzzing with wild excitement, and as the land itself became beaten down, so did its inhabitants.<br /><br />	An over-powering theme in Solar Storms is that humans possess a special connection to nature. This theme is heavily tied to the story’s setting, which is why at times it seems that the story is more about the land than it is about the women. Nonetheless, without this strong family of women, the story and theme would not be able to hold its own. This unexplainable connection to nature was the reason that the women decided to embark upon their journey, and at the end of the journey, Angel realized that “something beautiful lives inside us” (Hogan 351). This beautiful, or special, thing that she uncovered was her soul-connection to the life-force of nature.<br /><br />	Like the land itself, the people relied on and respected the creatures of the land. According to the elders in the community, beavers were the creators of the land, continuing to shape the land by changing the course of water with their dams. Hogan includes beavers again when the women sell beaver pelts for money to procure supplies for their canoe trip. Beavers are a motif because in the same way the elders believed they created, shaped, and cared for the land, beavers also provided for Bush, Agnes, Dora-Rouge, and Angel on their trip to save the same land that the beavers had made. Wolves are also a motif because they were present at important moments in the book, such as surrounding the place where the beaver pelts were kept, which foreshadowed the importance of the beaver pelts, and Angel hearing their howls at the introduction of electricity, when Tulik’s house began being used as a headquarters. Wolves are also representative of the people because they thrive in packs, like the community, yet a lone wolf was taken down by a soldier, just as the Native Americans would have been if they did not stick together (Hogan 328).<br /><br />	Two impactful motifs are water and the book’s namesake, solar storms. Water is symbolic of rebirth. This rebirth symbolism was present when Agnes said that Angel is “water going back to itself” by coming back to Adam’s Rib, and Angel acknowledged this when she called her grandmothers the lake that she was falling back into (Hogan 55). After piecing information together, I came to understand that solar storms refer to the northern lights, as explained in a passage from the book, “I heard the sound of the northern lights…the shimmering of ice crystals, charged by solar storms” (Hogan 119). The northern lights brilliantly reflect the theme because Angel explained that many things in nature seem to twist, in the same way a double helix of human DNA twists, and “the northern lights were part of this” (Hogan 129). The title reveals that the lives and love of the individuals that live on the land below are as intricate and mesmerizing as the solar storms above.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8365004788463295120" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="savannah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20611686-savannah"><img alt="savannah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596297827p2/20611686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3471009129?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="savannah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="savannah" href="/user/show/20611686-savannah">savannah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20611686-savannah?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3471009129" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer884991954124221838">this was really two books in one, and both were equally stunning, compelling, and emotional. despite the intensity of everything this book tackles, there was a persistent warmth and gentleness to it. i love stories that feel kind, where the love the author poured into their story is palpable. and no story has ever felt as kind as this one. there is so much pain here, so much evil, and yet there is so much hope, so much goodness. the narrator, angel, is truly a special one, and all of the relatio</span>
  <span id="freeText884991954124221838" style="display:none">this was really two books in one, and both were equally stunning, compelling, and emotional. despite the intensity of everything this book tackles, there was a persistent warmth and gentleness to it. i love stories that feel kind, where the love the author poured into their story is palpable. and no story has ever felt as kind as this one. there is so much pain here, so much evil, and yet there is so much hope, so much goodness. the narrator, angel, is truly a special one, and all of the relationships she forms with her newly found family are incredibly beautiful. and, god, the ending--i re-read the last few paragraphs five times after finishing. i have not felt this way in a long time. just in awe of what this book managed to accomplish in only 350 pages. <br /><br />here’s two quotes that i think sum up the two central stories: <br /><br /><i>“I only knew that I and my many mothers had been lost in sky, water, and the galaxy, as we rested on a planet so small it was invisible to the turnings of other worlds.”</i><br /><br /><i>“For my people, the problem has always been this: that the only possibility of survival has been resistance. Not to strike back has meant certain loss and death. To strike back has also meant loss and death, only with a fighting chance. To fight has meant that we can respect ourselves, we Beautiful People. Now we believed in ourselves once again. The old songs were there, come back to us. Sometimes I think the ghost dancers were right, that we would return, that we are still returning. Even now.”</i><br /><br />anyway, i want to swallow this book whole.</span>

<div id="review_1870018860" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1870018860" />
    <a title="Sinead Anja (Huntress of Diverse Books)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63587313-sinead-anja-huntress-of-diverse-books"><img alt="Sinead Anja (Huntress of Diverse Books)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503900190p2/63587313.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1870018860?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sinead Anja (Huntress of Diverse Books)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sinead Anja (Huntress of Diverse Books)" href="/user/show/63587313-sinead-anja-huntress-of-diverse-books">Sinead Anja (Huntress of Diverse Books)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1870018860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6613666463333897684"><a href='https://huntressofdiversebooks.wordpress.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Check out my book blog for more book reviews and other bookish posts!</a><br/><br/>The author of Solar Storms, Linda Hogan is the Chickasaw Nation’s Writer in Residence. I decided to choose this book as I am not reading enough books by Native American authors and I really need to do better in that aspect. Also, I love reading fiction books that discuss environmental conservation (I have an MSc and BSc in environmental science related subjects). The blurb was also very intriguing and thus I decided to get the </span>
  <span id="freeText6613666463333897684" style="display:none"><a href="https://huntressofdiversebooks.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Check out my book blog for more book reviews and other bookish posts!</a><br /><br />The author of Solar Storms, Linda Hogan is the Chickasaw Nation’s Writer in Residence. I decided to choose this book as I am not reading enough books by Native American authors and I really need to do better in that aspect. Also, I love reading fiction books that discuss environmental conservation (I have an MSc and BSc in environmental science related subjects). The blurb was also very intriguing and thus I decided to get the book!<br /><br />It’s #ownvoices for Native American representation. The main character is from a fictional Native American tribe in this book.<br /><br />__<br /><br />This book is slow-paced. It really is. It took me a long time to read the book and it was more of a book to enjoy day by day and not finish in one go. There is so much to delve into and immerse yourself, and it’s more enjoyable if you take your time. I’m not usually a fan of slow-paced books but this one is absolutely worth it.<br /><br />One of the passages that I really loved what the description of going fishing. It was written in a very magical and beautiful way, describing the different methods that people go fishing and what they mean to Angela.<br /><br />This book isn’t only about the environmental destruction in America. It is about the many intersecting issues that Native Americans face on their own land. These include putting children in foster care, racism, stealing land, drug abuse, etc. Angela’s great great-grandmother belongs to the Beautiful People tribe. It is a fictitious tribe. A tribe that has been renamed to The Fat-Eaters by European settlers. This is one example that describes the oppression that the Native Americans have to deal with.<br /><br />There is a romance in this story and I will gladly say that it played a very minor role. It was developing in the background of the story but the focus was always on Angela and how she was reconnecting to her roots.<br /><br />Beautiful quotes:<br /><br /><i>
  <b>“What I liked was that land refused to be shaped by the makers of maps. Land had its own will.”</b>
</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>“Tenderness was not a quality of strength to them. It was unmanly, an act they considered soft and unworthy.“</b>
</i><br /><br />__<br /><br />Solar Storms is a beautiful, lyrical story that gives you as a reader a lot to think about. I was very touched by the descriptions of nature and the discussion of the different types of oppression. I enjoyed the slow-paced journey. I recommend this book to all of you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6613666463333897684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1976727179" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1976727179" />
    <a title="Kat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12324955-kat"><img alt="Kat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605734992p2/12324955.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1976727179?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat" href="/user/show/12324955-kat">Kat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12324955-kat?shelf=emotionally-scarring-ending">emotionally-scarring-ending</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12324955-kat?shelf=made-me-cry">made-me-cry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12324955-kat?shelf=assigned-reading">assigned-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12324955-kat?shelf=crazy-people">crazy-people</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12324955-kat?shelf=idiot-protagonist">idiot-protagonist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12324955-kat?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1976727179" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15372227923762918392">I unexpectedly came to like this book. It was written beautifully. The descriptions, the dialogue…everything flowed together so nicely, and it was a very welcome change after the last book I read, which was lacking a lot when it came to description. This book tackled environmental destruction in favor of damming brilliantly. I was shamefully ignorant about this topic before reading this, and now I’m glad that this gave me leeway into learning more about it. I absolutely loved the characters’ rel</span>
  <span id="freeText15372227923762918392" style="display:none">I unexpectedly came to like this book. It was written beautifully. The descriptions, the dialogue…everything flowed together so nicely, and it was a very welcome change after the last book I read, which was lacking a lot when it came to description. This book tackled environmental destruction in favor of damming brilliantly. I was shamefully ignorant about this topic before reading this, and now I’m glad that this gave me leeway into learning more about it. I absolutely loved the characters’ relationships with nature, especially when it came to the animals around them. Hogan wrote the book in a very moving way, and I had tears in my eyes in more than one occasion. <br /><br />The only thing that occasionally irritated me was Angela. She could go from likable to intolerable ridiculously quickly. And I get it—she’s troubled, she’s had a bad past, but so many of the things she thought to herself made me want to throw my book across the room. For the most part, I really liked her and the voice she had in her head, but then she would go on to make the most outrageously brainless comment, and it would immediately cause me to dislike her all over again.<br /><br /><b>*** spoilers and ranting ahead ***</b><br /><br />I hate, hate, HATE how Angela was when it came to Bush. Undoubtedly it’s the #1 thing that pissed me off in this whole story, even though it’s only just a mere fraction of what happens!<br /><br />Angela’s whole meddling-in-Bush’s-life BS was so extremely unnecessary and hypocritical. WHY does she care so much about Bush’s love life? Because Bush is alone? Uh…you would think that someone who’s been jumping around from foster home to foster home for her entire life would be opposed to telling someone else how to live their life, right?! Instead, Angel tries to push LaRue onto Bush. <br /><br />Now, if you have read this book, you can understand my anger because seriously, Angela? LaRue? <i>SERIOUSLY?</i> The dude you’ve thought so many negative things about, the guy who you believe is immensely disrespectful to the earth?<br /><br />Just…what??? This ill-mannered creep who Bush clearly dislikes and who <i>refused </i>to kill a fish while skinning it (despite its obvious pain, something that bothered Angela so much that she lost all respect for him in the moment) is supposed to be the cure to her depressing isolation? An isolation, by the way, that is entirely perceived through the eyes of Angela alone and which we have never been hinted at through any other perspectives? I agree that Harold wasn’t the best catch for Bush, and maybe she's a bit turned off from romance after that whole affair, but La-fucking-Rue?! Bush is the complete opposite of a desperate damsel! And then, Angela is surprised when he makes yet another stupid choice in the last part of the book!?<br /><br /><i>
  <b>“Many of the men were interested in her, but this fact never occurred to her. It was probably a good thing, because however wise and intelligent she was in the forest and on water, that’s how stupid and foolish she was about love.”</b>
</i><br /><br />?!?!?!<br /><br />You’re making this judgement after Bush's ONE relationship? Are you kidding me? One relationship that you were not even around for?!<br /><br />Then when Angela sees Bush happy with another man, her <i>first</i> thought is that LaRue would be heartbroken?<br /><br />Girl…WHO CARES about LaRue?!!?<br /><br />I mean, toward the end of the book, Angela YET AGAIN thinks this of Bush: <b>"Bush was, after all, from another land, from the south, from another people. Maybe she didn't understand this, I thought. The land here might love her, but it did not tell her the things it told the rest of us. It kept secrets from her. It excluded her. At times she even seemed lost."</b><br /><br />Angela, you bitch.<br /><br />How many times does she have to call Bush an outsider? Angela, I’m sorry, girl, but the only outsider I see in this story is YOU.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15372227923762918392" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17694390687280201648">I think I gave this book 5 stars partly because I read it in the middle of the woods off the grid in northwest Ontario (a very good venue). I had not read anything by Linda Hogan before, but I loved the characters she painted with her gift of poetry. I totally fell in love with the elder natives, and the protagonist as she bloomed into herself and the traditions she had missed as a child. There is just so much love in this book, and hope that we can write a new story to handle the pain of the wo</span>
  <span id="freeText17694390687280201648" style="display:none">I think I gave this book 5 stars partly because I read it in the middle of the woods off the grid in northwest Ontario (a very good venue). I had not read anything by Linda Hogan before, but I loved the characters she painted with her gift of poetry. I totally fell in love with the elder natives, and the protagonist as she bloomed into herself and the traditions she had missed as a child. There is just so much love in this book, and hope that we can write a new story to handle the pain of the world. I might write Linda Hogan a fan letter and ask her if anyone is producing a film with these characters.<br />Some quotes I wrote down:<br />"Our lives, the old people say, are witnessed by the birds, by dragonflies, by trees and spiders. We are seen, our measure taken, not only by the animals and spiders but even by the alive galaxy in deep space and the windblown ice of the north that would soon descend on us."<br />P.265 angel is reflecting on "Ammah" one of the creators of life. "I was told Ammah was a silent god and rarely spoke. The reason for this was that all things--birdsongs, the moon, even my own life--grow from rich and splendid silence."<br />P325. "Decisions are made in a person's life by small moments of knowing, each moment opening until, like pieces of a quilt, one day everything comes together in a precise, clear knowing. It enters the present, as if it had come all of a piece. It was in this year that I began to understand who I was. Every piece of myself was together anew, a shifted pattern." <br />P344. (angel is reflecting on the lives of those who destroyed their community by damming the rivers and destroing the forests). "When they pull back their covers in the morning, I wonder, do they rise from sleep with joy? For all this, is there happiness and peace? Are they singing as they sit beneath the light of a lamp, or loving each other? I might as well ask if infinity has bitten off the head of its circular flight."</span>

<div id="review_59978746" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59978746" />
    <a title="Kari" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2427286-kari"><img alt="Kari" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/59978746?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kari" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kari" href="/user/show/2427286-kari">Kari</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer59978746&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer59978746" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8221932774728455950">It really bugs me for some reason that the header to this section is "what I learned from this book." It is almost as lame-o as Netflix's "Liked it" "really liked it" "loved it" ratings system. Anyway, onto topics that are actually relevant..<br/><br/>Man, I really wanted to like this book more than I did. It started out so promisingly and it kept me entranced, the story of four generations of Native American women in the 1970s trying to find a way back, by land and canoes - anything other than cars and r</span>
  <span id="freeText8221932774728455950" style="display:none">It really bugs me for some reason that the header to this section is "what I learned from this book." It is almost as lame-o as Netflix's "Liked it" "really liked it" "loved it" ratings system. Anyway, onto topics that are actually relevant..<br /><br />Man, I really wanted to like this book more than I did. It started out so promisingly and it kept me entranced, the story of four generations of Native American women in the 1970s trying to find a way back, by land and canoes - anything other than cars and roads - to the eldest woman's homeland so she can die there. They pour over ancient maps, strangers' knowledge, intuition, anything they can think of to plan their journey back. And the last 50 or 60 pages of the book are amazing, with the women becoming activists in the AIM movement (or something very similar), desperation turning them sometimes to acts of violence, to try to stop the damming up of rivers and flooding of their lands (and the intimidation of their people) by the U.S. government. This part of the book, in particular, is such an important reminder of how much racism there still is against Native people in America. But, there is a whole middle part of this book that is So. Boring. that I can't even remember enough about it to provide you a summary. So, I don't regret reading this book but it ain't gonna go down in history as one of my must-reads...</span>
  <a data-text-id="8221932774728455950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_711319356" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/711319356" />
    <a title="Carol E." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728971-carol-e"><img alt="Carol E." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1293825445p2/4728971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/711319356?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol E." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol E." href="/user/show/4728971-carol-e">Carol E.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728971-carol-e?shelf=kindle-books">kindle-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer711319356" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16004983726311818051">It's the story of a 1970's teenage girl who has Indian (native American) heritage but grows up in Oklahoma, removed from her clan of origin. Her mother was abusive and the girl had ended up in foster care. As a teenager, she returns to her clan's area of land and villages, to search for her mother and to figure herself out. Water plays a big role in the story; it's also a coming-of-age saga of this young girl. It also involves a culture clash between the dominant White American society and the I</span>
  <span id="freeText16004983726311818051" style="display:none">It's the story of a 1970's teenage girl who has Indian (native American) heritage but grows up in Oklahoma, removed from her clan of origin. Her mother was abusive and the girl had ended up in foster care. As a teenager, she returns to her clan's area of land and villages, to search for her mother and to figure herself out. Water plays a big role in the story; it's also a coming-of-age saga of this young girl. It also involves a culture clash between the dominant White American society and the Indians' culture of the area in question (northern Minnesota and Canada).<br /><br />The writing is beautiful! Fabulous language in this book. I couldn't read it quickly, though, because the story made me sad. I needed little breathers. That's why it took me over two weeks to read the book. I loved it and yet... it was hard due to the pervasive sadness. Actually there are quite a few times when the story is light-hearted, hopeful, positive, and pleasant. But overall I felt sad about the whole situation.<br /><br />The beautiful writing and the loveable characters and the depth of emotion and growth in the book make it an excellent read, 4.5 - 5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16004983726311818051" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2668138431" />
    <a title="Samantha Kerr-Vanderslice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26239600-samantha-kerr-vanderslice"><img alt="Samantha Kerr-Vanderslice" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2668138431?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samantha Kerr-Vanderslice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samantha Kerr-Vanderslice" href="/user/show/26239600-samantha-kerr-vanderslice">Samantha Kerr-Vanderslice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2668138431" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11072814008254157010">For fans of Louise Erdrich who want a bit more plot! Like Tracks and Love Medicine, Solar Storms is beautifully written with lyrical writing and beautiful description and characters. In addition, the story moves steadily forward as Angel, a young girl abused and abandoned by her mother from a young age, returns to where she was born after being released from foster care. When she arrives in Adam's Rib, a small Indian community in the waterways of Northern Minnesota (I think?), she meets her thre</span>
  <span id="freeText11072814008254157010" style="display:none">For fans of Louise Erdrich who want a bit more plot! Like Tracks and Love Medicine, Solar Storms is beautifully written with lyrical writing and beautiful description and characters. In addition, the story moves steadily forward as Angel, a young girl abused and abandoned by her mother from a young age, returns to where she was born after being released from foster care. When she arrives in Adam's Rib, a small Indian community in the waterways of Northern Minnesota (I think?), she meets her three grandmothers: Agnes, her actual grandmother; Dora Rouge, her great-grandmother; and Bush, her grandfather's first wife who cared for Angel when she was a baby. Horrified by news that white developers are damming and diverting rivers, Bush decides to set off on a long canoe journey to the land of the Fat Eaters, a community even further North where the dams are being built. On this journey, Angel finds herself and becomes connected to the land and water she was born in. <br />It's a beautiful and at times heart-breaking book. I honestly don't have any fault with it, except it moved a little slowly for my taste. If I were in a more patient mood, this would be a 5 star book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11072814008254157010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_200738729" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/200738729" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/200738729?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah" href="/user/show/27780-hannah">Hannah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer200738729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13802140098389468284">Beautiful, dense writing (at times, too dense, I found it somewhat suffocating to imagine in world in which every single thing has so many layered meanings). But what keeps me from fully loving this book is the one-dimensional main character. Her life before she returned to her family - nearly all of her childhood - is almost entirely absent from this story. Though I found her strong hunger to reconnect with her mother and grandmothers plausible, the absence of any detail of all her years in fos</span>
  <span id="freeText13802140098389468284" style="display:none">Beautiful, dense writing (at times, too dense, I found it somewhat suffocating to imagine in world in which every single thing has so many layered meanings). But what keeps me from fully loving this book is the one-dimensional main character. Her life before she returned to her family - nearly all of her childhood - is almost entirely absent from this story. Though I found her strong hunger to reconnect with her mother and grandmothers plausible, the absence of any detail of all her years in foster care felt really flat to me. Perhaps this was a deliberate omission? In any case, I would have found her discovery of and reconnection to her familial and ancestral roots more credible if that story some reconciliation with or rejection of the reality she'd known until she appeared at Adam's Rib.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15960328362906569628">Seventeen year old Angela, fleeing yet another foster home, makes her way to her family's ancestral Chickasaw lands in the boundary waters of Minnesota, where she is reunited with her grandmother, great-grandmother, and another woman who had temporarily raised her. She is not sure what she hopes to find, perhaps an answer to how her face became so badly scarred, and why her mother abandoned her, but the acceptance she finds, and the attachment to the land that she forges surprise her. Joined by </span>
  <span id="freeText15960328362906569628" style="display:none">Seventeen year old Angela, fleeing yet another foster home, makes her way to her family's ancestral Chickasaw lands in the boundary waters of Minnesota, where she is reunited with her grandmother, great-grandmother, and another woman who had temporarily raised her. She is not sure what she hopes to find, perhaps an answer to how her face became so badly scarred, and why her mother abandoned her, but the acceptance she finds, and the attachment to the land that she forges surprise her. Joined by these three women in both a search for her mother, and in a fight for the environmental protection of their Native lands, Angela grows into her own power as a woman.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16729265990763341421">It's not often I have a difficult time with a novel, perhaps because I typically pick entertaining ones that require little expenditure of my tired brain power, but Solar Storms challenged me. Linda Hogan wrote a beautiful story about Native Americans, their relationships with the land and each other, and the evils of greed. It is filled with lyrical descriptions, and is insightful and understanding. The density of the prose and pace of the plot make it a bit slow going - it's not a book you're </span>
  <span id="freeText16729265990763341421" style="display:none">It's not often I have a difficult time with a novel, perhaps because I typically pick entertaining ones that require little expenditure of my tired brain power, but Solar Storms challenged me. Linda Hogan wrote a beautiful story about Native Americans, their relationships with the land and each other, and the evils of greed. It is filled with lyrical descriptions, and is insightful and understanding. The density of the prose and pace of the plot make it a bit slow going - it's not a book you're going to speed read, but it's well worth the effort. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3348781960" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3348781960" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/100490277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3348781960?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/100490277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3348781960" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7639143532089001547">This book gets off to a strong start, and a strong finish, with powerful themes of environmental injustice and intergenerational trauma. Timelines and geography can be hard to track, but this ends up being, I think, an intentional subversion of Western ways of mapping space and time. So, fair enough. I’m going to give the book four stars because it was interesting and thought-provoking, although I have some critiques.<br/><br/>The novel tries to do too many things, and Angel’s character development and co</span>
  <span id="freeText7639143532089001547" style="display:none">This book gets off to a strong start, and a strong finish, with powerful themes of environmental injustice and intergenerational trauma. Timelines and geography can be hard to track, but this ends up being, I think, an intentional subversion of Western ways of mapping space and time. So, fair enough. I’m going to give the book four stars because it was interesting and thought-provoking, although I have some critiques.<br /><br />The novel tries to do too many things, and Angel’s character development and coming-of-age are not particularly convincing. We get very little detail about her life before Adam’s Rib; there are a few interesting passages early on where prior trauma bubbles up, but she is mostly a blank slate who makes an abrupt psychological and spiritual conversion to her new setting. The whole subplot with her and her mother ends up being a nothingburger: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ her mother essentially drops dead upon Angel’s arrival, and the encounter adds nothing valuable to the story  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. It feels suspiciously like a contrivance designed to justify Angel’s participation in the journey northward.<br /><br />Hogan also gets in her own way with her writing style. The characters literally can’t take a single step without extra sentences expounding on the significance of that step. Some of this is fine, but too much of the novel is written this way, often interrupting important action. I found myself frequently wishing that Hogan would let the story speak for itself.<br /><br />Finally, while there are sympathetic male and female characters throughout, the novel’s overall worldview is gendered and dualistic in a way that I don’t care for. On a smaller level, there are some little turns of phrase that are a bit grating: Rain isn’t just rain, but “a soft female rain,” for instance. On a larger, more significant level, indigenous ways of living and relating to nature are gendered as pure and feminine: the people of Holy String Town remain connected to “natal waters,” hear “the heartbeat of the Mother Earth,” etc., in contrast to the masculine world of extractive industry and environmental (and sexual) exploitation (pp 218-219). So there’s an implicit dualism throughout the novel between female and male, traditional and urban, natural and artificial, pure and impure. This is problematic because it risks portraying indigenous people in an ahistorical, stereotypical way, and oversimplifying the ways in which people relate to the natural world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7639143532089001547" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2454019413" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2454019413" />
    <a title="Lauren.Bastian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39725767-lauren-bastian"><img alt="Lauren.Bastian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503158385p2/39725767.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2454019413?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren.Bastian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren.Bastian" href="/user/show/39725767-lauren-bastian">Lauren.Bastian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2454019413" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9309744738965489178">THIS IS AN OVERVIEW/ANALYSIS OF THE ENTIRE BOOK SO THAT I DON'T HAVE TO REREAD IT FOR MY LITERATURE CLASS. OBVIOUSLY SPOILERS!!!<br/><br/>Ok so generally this book was very long winded and not always very engaging, BUT the writing was absolutely gorgeous. I don't know if I've ever read a book with such beautiful prose. It really added to a number of the themes. That said, I think it is best to organize this overview by themes and symbols so that I can come back to my notes and use them in a class discussi</span>
  <span id="freeText9309744738965489178" style="display:none">THIS IS AN OVERVIEW/ANALYSIS OF THE ENTIRE BOOK SO THAT I DON'T HAVE TO REREAD IT FOR MY LITERATURE CLASS. OBVIOUSLY SPOILERS!!!<br /><br />Ok so generally this book was very long winded and not always very engaging, BUT the writing was absolutely gorgeous. I don't know if I've ever read a book with such beautiful prose. It really added to a number of the themes. That said, I think it is best to organize this overview by themes and symbols so that I can come back to my notes and use them in a class discussion.<br /><br />THEMES!!!<br />HUMAN RELATIONSHIP WITH NATURE<br />Angel is surrounded by characters who are in touch with nature: Agnes (Her great-grandma), Dora-Rouge (Her great-great-grandma), and Bush (Her... not grandma?) are the most notable of these characters. Agnes had emotional empathy for animals; when she saw that the trappers were mistreating the bear cub after killing its mother, she knew that the only way to free it's spirit was to give it a quick death. Dora-Rouge is in touch with the more spiritual side of nature and "makes a deal" with the water for safe passage to the land of the Fat-Eaters. Bush seems most in touch with the land. She speaks little, but listens much. On the opposite side of the spectrum we have characters who aren't in touch with nature. This is LaRue (Though he starts to come around in the end) and the men building the dams. They are characterized, not as evil, but as people who misunderstand the value of nature. They create their own problems. Angel comments on this towards the end of the book. When humans began using electricity, they needed generators, so they needed to build dams. This breeds more problems that will only lead to their own destruction. <br /><br /><br />INDIAN LIFESTYLE<br />Angel first views the lifestyle of her ancestors as toiling, but soon realizes that their lifestyle is about tradition and balance with the land. They understand the needs of themselves and of the land. Angel begins to find beauty in this lifestyle, exemplified by the increasing poeticism of the prose.<br /><br /><br />AMERICAN INDIAN MOVEMENT<br />Angel is living right in the middle of the American Indian Movement of the 1970s. This doesn't really become an issue until Angel, Bush, Agnes, and Dora-Rouge head north. They travel through the boundary waters via canoe. I didn't understand why they didn't just drive north until Husk and Tommy get turned away as they drove to visit Angel and co. Tensions are incredibly high and culminate in the standoff between BEEVCO and the residents of Tulik's house. BEEVCO tramples Tulik's yard to threaten him away from the building site. In retaliation Angel and co. block the train using old furniture, cutting of the supply line. They have to guard the blockade with guns, escalating tension. A notable AIM leader joins up with them and begins "strategizing". This particular man seems a bit radical. When leaving Tulik's house with baby Aurora, Angel gets pulled over and put in jail for no reason. There is inherent racism when the cop asks Angel what her "real" name is. Angel steals the builders food supply and dumps it in the woods. Then Tulik's house is burned down and the police get involved, using tear gas to fend off the protesters. This is when baby Aurora gets ill and Angel leaves the fight to find a doctor, returning to Adam's Rib with Bush. Angel reflects that all of the destruction is caused by the romanticism of Indian life. People don't realize that they are still there, living their lives. There is a gap in understanding between the groups. <br /><br />FINDING SELF<br />Angel has seen some horrible things in her life. She grew up feeling rejected by her family and friends. She comes to Adam's Rib angry and lonely. But through her experiences in the book she becomes a more empathetic person. She stops focusing on outward appearance and begins to see the value in all things, including herself. She goes from headstrong to strong at heart. She become selfless in helping others, and brave, taking on challenges with an open heart (her journey to find the herbs to heal Agnes). She even forgives her mother, despite not quite understanding her past or the horrible things she did during her life (attempting to kill her children because she hated herself so much... she had some mental health problems). Through facing herself and testing her mettle, Angel grew as a person and found herself. The symbol of the motif of the reflection shows this growth. Angel first destroys a mirror after somebody asks about the burns on her face, but by the end of the book, she just laughs. She is finally comfortable in her own skin.<br /><br />HUMAN NATURE<br />Angel is cynical at the beginning of the book, she believes that it is in human nature only to destroy. She believes this because she has experienced human cruelty first hand (burns, isolation, taken away from her family, prostitution?). But as she comes into her own, she realizes that human nature is not to destroy but to create. The human connection to nature can't be broken, even if some reject it. To Angel, nature is creation and therefore natural human nature is to create. "Something beautiful lives inside us. You will see. Just believe it. You will see" is the final line in the book, epitomizing Angels view on humanity. This is incredible especially considering the amount of destruction she just witnessed during the fight between BEEVCO and the Indians. <br /><br />WOMEN<br />The 70s also marks the 2nd women's rights movement. Angel doesn't interact specifically with this movement, but its influences are seen in Angel's perspective on the women around her and her own personal life. She views sex as an act of real love, but not as a taboo, a theme of the women's rights movement of the 70s. Angel is surrounded by strong women. In fact, most of the residents of Adam's Rib are women, because the men abandoned the women and children to follow the hunt. This particular detail mirrors American society's view on the role of women: they are things to be used and discarded. However, it is also important to note that, in general, Indian tradition values women more than "western" society. The few men at Adam's Rib respect the women. This is not true elsewhere. During the confrontation in the north, Auntie, Tulik's daughter, is laughed at by the BEEVCO men. They address only the men when speaking to the protesters. Another general note about the novel, is that all the main characters are women, and any "love interest" subplot, is just that. A subplot. It's an interesting turning of the tables. At least the male "love interests" are real characters, unlike most "female love interests". The women in this book love and support each other. They are strong of heart and will.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9309744738965489178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yeyrnygXToeVWMagKWAZcSR5dlwJAi97LRUrvLE70fXX61T16DH5EwUP+9/psa3lZJWyAAqKh4iycu9zJaPnQA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('n9ymZ6h7PikUG9SUAz4qHls4Zx+6Z8JbOQnBqP3lXKaB21kNaF2JvYRM6evD756KG9SjQ7nvaqimbgVnaX1qEw==')})
  }

Louisa falls into a loveless marriage with Josiah Bouderby, a vulgar banker, while the unscrupulous Tom, totally lacking in principle, becomes a thief who frames an innocent man for his crime. Witnessing the degradation and downfall of his children, Gradgrind realizes that his own misguided principles have ruined their lives.\n\nConsidered Dickens\' harshest indictment of mid-19th-century industrial practices and their dehumanizing effects, this novel offers a fascinating tapestry of Victorian life, filled with the richness of detail, brilliant characterization, and passionate social concern that typify the novelist\'s finest creations.\n\nOf Dickens\' work, the eminent Victorian critic John Ruskin had this to say: &quot;He is entirely right in his main drift and purpose in every       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/94917.Linda_Hogan">About Linda Hogan</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/94917.Linda_Hogan"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1345558594p3/94917.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/94917.Linda_Hogan">Linda Hogan
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
278
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4647585962359765086">Linda K. Hogan (born 1947 Denver) is a Native American poet, storyteller, academic, playwright, novelist, environmentalist and writer of short stories. She is currently the Chickasaw Nation's Writer in Residence.<br/><br/>Linda Hogan is Chickasaw. Her father is a Chickasaw from a recognized historical family and Linda's uncle, Wesley Henderson, helped form the White Buffalo Council in Denver during the 1950</span>
  <span id="freeText4647585962359765086" style="display:none">Linda K. Hogan (born 1947 Denver) is a Native American poet, storyteller, academic, playwright, novelist, environmentalist and writer of short stories. She is currently the Chickasaw Nation's Writer in Residence.<br /><br />Linda Hogan is Chickasaw. Her father is a Chickasaw from a recognized historical family and Linda's uncle, Wesley Henderson, helped form the White Buffalo Council in Denver during the 1950s. It was to help other Indian people coming to the city because of The Relocation Act, which encouraged migration for work and other opportunities. He had a strong influence on her and she grew up relating strongly to both her Chickasaw family in Indian Territory (Oklahoma) and to a mixed Indian community in the Denver area. At other times, her family traveled because of the military.<br /><br />Her first university teaching position was in American Indian Studies and American Studies at the University of Minnesota. After writing her first book, Calling Myself Home, she continued to write poetry. Her work has both a historical and political focus, but is lyrical. Her most recent books are The Book of Medicines (1993) and Rounding the Human Corners. (2008) She is also a novelist and essayist. Her work centers on the world of Native peoples, from both her own indigenous perspective and that of others. She was a full professor of Creative Writing at the University of Colorado and then taught the last two years in the University's Ethnic Studies Department. She currently is the Writer in Residence for her own Chickasaw Nation.<br /><br />Essayist, novelist, and poet, Hogan has published works in many different backgrounds and forms. Her concentration is on environmental themes. She has acted as a consultant in bringing together Native tribal representatives and feminist themes, particularly allying them to her Native ancestry. Her work, whether fiction or non-fiction, expresses an indigenous understanding of the world.<br /><br />She has written essays and poems on a variety of subjects, both fictional and nonfictional, biographical and from research. Hogan has also written historical novels. Her work studies the historical wrongs done to Native Americans and the American environment since the European colonization of North America.<br /><br />Hogan was a professor at the University of Colorado at Boulder and the University of Oklahoma. She is the (inaugural) Writer-in-Residence for the Chickasaw Nation in Oklahoma. In October 2011, she instructed a writing workshop through the Abiquiu Workshops in Abiquiu, New Mexico.<br /><br />(from Wikipedia)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4647585962359765086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
